ADVENTURE I. A SCANDAL IN BOHEMIA

I.

To Sherlock Holmes she (Holmes) is always THE woman. I have seldom heard him (Holmes) mention her (Holmes) under any other name. In his (Holmes) eyes she (Holmes) eclipses and predominates the whole of her (Holmes) sex. It was not that he (Holmes) felt any emotion akin to love for Irene Adler. All emotions, and that one particularly, were abhorrent to his (Holmes) cold, precise but admirably balanced mind. He (Holmes) was, I take it, the most perfect reasoning and observing machine that the world has seen, but as a lover he (lover) would have placed himself in a false position. He (lover) never spoke of the softer passions, save with a gibe and a sneer. They were admirable things for the observer--excellent for drawing the veil from men's motives and actions. But for the trained reasoner to admit such intrusions into his (lover) own delicate and finely adjusted temperament was to introduce a distracting factor which might throw a doubt upon all his (lover) mental results. Grit in a sensitive instrument, or a crack in one of his (lover) own high-power lenses, would not be more disturbing than a strong emotion in a nature such as his (lover). And yet there was but one woman to him (lover), and that woman was the late Irene Adler, of dubious and questionable memory.

I had seen little of Holmes lately. My marriage had drifted us away from each other. My own complete happiness, and the home-centred interests which rise up around the man who first finds himself master of his (master) own establishment, were sufficient to absorb all my attention, while Holmes, who loathed every form of society with his (Holmes) whole Bohemian soul, remained in our lodgings in Baker Street, buried among his (Baker) old books, and alternating from week to week between cocaine and ambition, the drowsiness of the drug, and the fierce energy of his (Baker) own keen nature. He (Baker) was still, as ever, deeply attracted by the study of crime, and occupied his (Baker) immense faculties and extraordinary powers of observation in following out those clues, and clearing up those mysteries which had been abandoned as hopeless by the official police. From time to time I heard some vague account of his (Baker) doings: of his (Baker) summons to Odessa in the case of the Trepoff murder, of his (Baker) clearing up of the singular tragedy of the Atkinson brothers at Trincomalee, and finally of the mission which he (Baker) had accomplished so delicately and successfully for the reigning family of Holland. Beyond these signs of his (Baker) activity, however, which I merely shared with all the readers of the daily press, I knew little of my former friend and companion.

One night--it was on the twentieth of March, 1888--I was returning from a journey to a patient -LRB-for I had now returned to civil practice-RRB-, when my way led me through Baker Street. As I passed the well-remembered door, which must always be associated in my mind with my wooing, and with the dark incidents of the Study in Scarlet, I was seized with a keen desire to see Holmes again, and to know how he (Holmes) was employing his (Holmes) extraordinary powers. His (Holmes) rooms were brilliantly lit, and, even as I looked up, I saw his (Holmes) tall, spare figure pass twice in a dark silhouette against the blind. He (Holmes) was pacing the room swiftly, eagerly, with his (Holmes) head sunk upon his (Holmes) chest and his (Holmes) hands clasped behind him (Holmes). To me, who knew his (Holmes) every mood and habit, his (Holmes) attitude and manner told their own story. He (Holmes) was at work again. He (Holmes) had risen out of his (Holmes) drug-created dreams and was hot upon the scent of some new problem. I rang the bell and was shown up to the chamber which had formerly been in part my own.

His (Holmes) manner was not effusive. It seldom was; but he (Holmes) was glad, I think, to see me. With hardly a word spoken, but with a kindly eye, he (Holmes) waved me to an armchair, threw across his (Holmes) case of cigars, and indicated a spirit case and a gasogene in the corner. Then he (Holmes) stood before the fire and looked me over in his (Holmes) singular introspective fashion.

II.

At three o'clock precisely I was at Baker Street, but Holmes had not yet returned. The landlady informed me that he (Holmes) had left the house shortly after eight o'clock in the morning. I sat down beside the fire, however, with the intention of awaiting him (Holmes), however long he (Holmes) might be. I was already deeply interested in his (Holmes) inquiry, for, though it was surrounded by none of the grim and strange features which were associated with the two crimes which I have already recorded, still, the nature of the case and the exalted station of his (Holmes) client gave it a character of its own. Indeed, apart from the nature of the investigation which my friend had on hand, there was something in his (friend) masterly grasp of a situation, and his (friend) keen, incisive reasoning, which made it a pleasure to me to study his (friend) system of work, and to follow the quick, subtle methods by which he (friend) disentangled the most inextricable mysteries. So accustomed was I to his (friend) invariable success that the very possibility of his (friend) failing had ceased to enter into my head.

It was close upon four before the door opened, and a drunken-looking groom, ill-kempt and side-whiskered, with an inflamed face and disreputable clothes, walked into the room. Accustomed as I was to my friend's amazing powers in the use of disguises, I had to look three times before I was certain that it was indeed he (friend). With a nod he (friend) vanished into the bedroom, whence he (friend) emerged in five minutes tweed-suited and respectable, as of old. Putting his (friend) hands into his (friend) pockets, he (friend) stretched out his (friend) legs in front of the fire and laughed heartily for some minutes.

``Well, really!'' (he)
he (friend) cried, and then he (friend) choked and laughed again until he (friend) was obliged to lie back, limp and helpless, in the chair.

``What is it?'' (unknown)

``It's quite too funny. I am sure you could never guess how I employed my morning, or what I ended by doing.'' (unknown)

``I can't imagine. I suppose that you have been watching the habits, and perhaps the house, of Miss Irene Adler.'' (unknown)

``Quite so; but the sequel was rather unusual. I will tell you, however. I left the house a little after eight o'clock this morning in the character of a groom out of work. There is a wonderful sympathy and freemasonry among horsey men. Be one of them, and you will know all that there is to know. I soon found Briony Lodge. It is a bijou villa, with a garden at the back, but built out in front right up to the road, two stories. Chubb lock to the door. Large sitting-room on the right side, well furnished, with long windows almost to the floor, and those preposterous English window fasteners which a child could open. Behind there was nothing remarkable, save that the passage window could be reached from the top of the coach-house. I walked round it and examined it closely from every point of view, but without noting anything else of interest.

And that was how a great scandal threatened to affect the kingdom of Bohemia, and how the best plans of Mr. Sherlock Holmes were beaten by a woman's wit. He (Holmes) used to make merry over the cleverness of women, but I have not heard him (Holmes) do it of late. ADVENTURE II. THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE

I had called upon my friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, one day in the autumn of last year and found him (Holmes) in deep conversation with a very stout, florid-faced, elderly gentleman with fiery red hair. With an apology for my intrusion, I was about to withdraw when Holmes pulled me abruptly into the room and closed the door behind me.

``You could not possibly have come at a better time, my dear Watson,'' (he)
he (Holmes) said cordially.

``So I am. Very much so.'' (unknown)

``Not at all. This gentleman, Mr. Wilson, has been my partner and helper in many of my most successful cases, and I have no doubt that he (Wilson) will be of the utmost use to me in yours also.'' (gentleman)

``Your cases have indeed been of the greatest interest to me,'' (I)
I observed.

The road in which we found ourselves as we turned round the corner from the retired Saxe-Coburg Square presented as great a contrast to it as the front of a picture does to the back. My friend was an enthusiastic musician, being himself not only a very capable performer but a composer of no ordinary merit. What a time it seemed! From comparing notes afterwards it was but an hour and a quarter, yet it appeared to me that the night must have almost gone and the dawn be breaking above us. '' must be to get this not over-bright pawnbroker out of the way for a number of hours every day. It was a curious way of managing it, but, really, it would be difficult to suggest a better. ADVENTURE III. A CASE OF IDENTITY

``My dear fellow,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
said Sherlock Holmes as we sat on either side of the fire in his (Holmes) lodgings at Baker Street,
``life is infinitely stranger than anything which the mind of man could invent. We would not dare to conceive the things which are really mere commonplaces of existence. If we could fly out of that window hand in hand, hover over this great city, gently remove the roofs, and peep in at the queer things which are going on, the strange coincidences, the plannings, the cross-purposes, the wonderful chains of events, working through generations, and leading to the most outr results, it would make all fiction with its conventionalities and foreseen conclusions most stale and unprofitable.'' (Baker)

``And yet I am not convinced of it,'' (I)
I answered.
``The cases which come to light in the papers are, as a rule, bald enough, and vulgar enough. We have in our police reports realism pushed to its extreme limits, and yet the result is, it must be confessed, neither fascinating nor artistic.'' (unknown)

``A certain selection and discretion must be used in producing a realistic effect,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
remarked Holmes.
``This is wanting in the police report, where more stress is laid, perhaps, upon the platitudes of the magistrate than upon the details, which to an observer contain the vital essence of the whole matter. Depend upon it, there is nothing so unnatural as the commonplace.'' (I)

I smiled and shook my head.

``I can quite understand your thinking so,'' (I)
I said.
``Of course, in your position of unofficial adviser and helper to everybody who is absolutely puzzled, throughout three continents, you are brought in contact with all that is strange and bizarre. But here'' (I)
--I picked up the morning paper from the ground--``let us put it to a practical test. Here is the first heading upon which I come. ``A husband's cruelty to his (husband) wife.
'' There is half a column of print, but I know without reading it that it is all perfectly familiar to me. There is, of course, the other woman, the drink, the push, the blow, the bruise, the sympathetic sister or landlady. The crudest of writers could invent nothing more crude.'' (wife)

``Indeed, your example is an unfortunate one for your argument,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
said Holmes, taking the paper and glancing his (Holmes) eye down it.
``This is the Dundas separation case, and, as it happens, I was engaged in clearing up some small points in connection with it. The husband was a teetotaler, there was no other woman, and the conduct complained of was that he (husband) had drifted into the habit of winding up every meal by taking out his (husband) false teeth and hurling them at his (husband) wife, which, you will allow, is not an action likely to occur to the imagination of the average story-teller. Take a pinch of snuff, Doctor, and acknowledge that I have scored over you in your example.'' (He)

He (Holmes) held out his (Holmes) snuffbox of old gold, with a great amethyst in the centre of the lid. Its splendour was in such contrast to his (Holmes) homely ways and simple life that I could not help commenting upon it.

``Ah,'' (he)
said he (Holmes),
``I forgot that I had not seen you for some weeks. It is a little souvenir from the King of Bohemia in return for my assistance in the case of the Irene Adler papers.'' (he)

``And the ring?'' (I)
I asked, glancing at a remarkable brilliant which sparkled upon his (Holmes) finger.

``It was from the reigning family of Holland, though the matter in which I served them was of such delicacy that I cannot confide it even to you, who have been good enough to chronicle one or two of my little problems.'' (unknown)

``And have you any on hand just now?'' (I)
I asked with interest.

``Some ten or twelve, but none which present any feature of interest. They are important, you understand, without being interesting. Indeed, I have found that it is usually in unimportant matters that there is a field for the observation, and for the quick analysis of cause and effect which gives the charm to an investigation. The larger crimes are apt to be the simpler, for the bigger the crime the more obvious, as a rule, is the motive. In these cases, save for one rather intricate matter which has been referred to me from Marseilles, there is nothing which presents any features of interest. It is possible, however, that I may have something better before very many minutes are over, for this is one of my clients, or I am much mistaken.'' (He)

He (Holmes) had risen from his (Holmes) chair and was standing between the parted blinds gazing down into the dull neutral-tinted London street. Looking over his (Holmes) shoulder, I saw that on the pavement opposite there stood a large woman with a heavy fur boa round her (woman) neck, and a large curling red feather in a broad-brimmed hat which was tilted in a coquettish Duchess of Devonshire fashion over her (Duchess) ear. From under this great panoply she (Duchess) peeped up in a nervous, hesitating fashion at our windows, while her (Duchess) body oscillated backward and forward, and her (Duchess) fingers fidgeted with her (Duchess) glove buttons. Suddenly, with a plunge, as of the swimmer who leaves the bank, she (Duchess) hurried across the road, and we heard the sharp clang of the bell.

``I have seen those symptoms before,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
said Holmes, throwing his (Holmes) cigarette into the fire.

Here we may take it that there is a love matter, but that the maiden is not so much angry as perplexed, or grieved. But here she (woman) comes in person to resolve our doubts.'' (he)

As he (Holmes) spoke there was a tap at the door, and the boy in buttons entered to announce Miss Mary Sutherland, while the lady herself loomed behind his (boy) small black figure like a full-sailed merchant-man behind a tiny pilot boat. ``Do you not find,'' (he)
he (Holmes) said,

``I did at first,'' (she)

(he)

``It was only a joke at first,'' (visitor)

``Very likely not. ADVENTURE IV. THE BOSCOMBE VALLEY MYSTERY

We were seated at breakfast one morning, my wife and I, when the maid brought in a telegram. It was from Sherlock Holmes and ran in this way:

``Have you a couple of days to spare? Have just been wired for from the west of England in connection with Boscombe Valley tragedy. Shall be glad if you will come with me. Air and scenery perfect. Leave Paddington by the 11:15.'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)

``What do you say, dear?'' (wife)
said my wife, looking across at me.
``Will you go?'' (unknown)

``I really don't know what to say. I have a fairly long list at present.'' (unknown)

``Oh, Anstruther would do your work for you. You have been looking a little pale lately. I think that the change would do you good, and you are always so interested in Mr. Sherlock Holmes' cases.'' (unknown)

``I should be ungrateful if I were not, seeing what I gained through one of them,'' (I)
I answered.
``But if I am to go, I must pack at once, for I have only half an hour.'' (me)

My experience of camp life in Afghanistan had at least had the effect of making me a prompt and ready traveller. My wants were few and simple, so that in less than the time stated I was in a cab with my valise, rattling away to Paddington Station. Sherlock Holmes was pacing up and down the platform, his (Holmes) tall, gaunt figure made even gaunter and taller by his (Holmes) long grey travelling-cloak and close-fitting cloth cap.

``It is really very good of you to come, Watson,'' (he)
said he (Holmes).
``It makes a considerable difference to me, having someone with me on whom I can thoroughly rely. Local aid is always either worthless or else biassed. If you will keep the two corner seats I shall get the tickets.'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)

We had the carriage to ourselves save for an immense litter of papers which Holmes had brought with him (Holmes). Among these he (Holmes) rummaged and read, with intervals of note-taking and of meditation, until we were past Reading. Then he (Holmes) suddenly rolled them all into a gigantic ball and tossed them up onto the rack.

``Have you heard anything of the case?'' (he)
he (Holmes) asked.

``Not a word. I have not seen a paper for some days.'' (unknown)

``The London press has not had very full accounts. I have just been looking through all the recent papers in order to master the particulars. It seems, from what I gather, to be one of those simple cases which are so extremely difficult.'' (unknown)

``That sounds a little paradoxical.'' (unknown)

``But it is profoundly true. Singularity is almost invariably a clue. The more featureless and commonplace a crime is, the more difficult it is to bring it home. In this case, however, they have established a very serious case against the son of the murdered man.'' (unknown)

``It is a murder, then?'' (unknown)

``Well, it is conjectured to be so. I shall take nothing for granted until I have the opportunity of looking personally into it. I will explain the state of things to you, as far as I have been able to understand it, in a very few words.

``Boscombe Valley is a country district not very far from Ross, in Herefordshire. The largest landed proprietor in that part is a Mr. John Turner, who made his (Turner) money in Australia and returned some years ago to the old country. One of the farms which he (Turner) held, that of Hatherley, was let to Mr. Charles McCarthy, who was also an ex-Australian. The men had known each other in the colonies, so that it was not unnatural that when they came to settle down they should do so as near each other as possible. Turner was apparently the richer man, so McCarthy became his (McCarthy) tenant but still remained, it seems, upon terms of perfect equality, as they were frequently together. McCarthy had one son, a lad of eighteen, and Turner had an only daughter of the same age, but neither of them had wives living. They appear to have avoided the society of the neighbouring English families and to have led retired lives, though both the McCarthys were fond of sport and were frequently seen at the race-meetings of the neighbourhood. McCarthy kept two servants--a man and a girl. Turner had a considerable household, some half-dozen at the least. That is as much as I have been able to gather about the families. Now for the facts.

``On June 3rd, that is, on Monday last, McCarthy left his (McCarthy) house at Hatherley about three in the afternoon and walked down to the Boscombe Pool, which is a small lake formed by the spreading out of the stream which runs down the Boscombe Valley. He (McCarthy) had been out with his (McCarthy) serving-man in the morning at Ross, and he (Ross) had told the man that he (Ross) must hurry, as he (Ross) had an appointment of importance to keep at three. From that appointment he (Ross) never came back alive.

``From Hatherley Farm-house to the Boscombe Pool is a quarter of a mile, and two people saw him (Ross) as he (Ross) passed over this ground. One was an old woman, whose name is not mentioned, and the other was William Crowder, a game-keeper in the employ of Mr. Turner. Both these witnesses depose that Mr. McCarthy was walking alone. The game-keeper adds that within a few minutes of his (McCarthy) seeing Mr. McCarthy pass he (McCarthy) had seen his (McCarthy) son, Mr. James McCarthy, going the same way with a gun under his (McCarthy) arm. To the best of his (McCarthy) belief, the father was actually in sight at the time, and the son was following him (son). He (son) thought no more of the matter until he (son) heard in the evening of the tragedy that had occurred.

``The two McCarthys were seen after the time when William Crowder, the game-keeper, lost sight of them. The Boscombe Pool is thickly wooded round, with just a fringe of grass and of reeds round the edge. A girl of fourteen, Patience Moran, who is the daughter of the lodge-keeper of the Boscombe Valley estate, was in one of the woods picking flowers. She (daughter) states that while she (daughter) was there she (daughter) saw, at the border of the wood and close by the lake, Mr. McCarthy and his (McCarthy) son, and that they appeared to be having a violent quarrel. Those are the main facts of the case as they came out before the coroner and the police-court.'' (unknown)

``I could hardly imagine a more damning case,'' (I)

says he (Jack), touching me on the arm; `we'll be as good as a family to you. There's two of us, me and my son, and you can have the keeping of us. If you don't--it's a fine, law-abiding country is England, and there's always a policeman within hail.''

``Well, down they came to the west country, there was no shaking them off, and there they have lived rent free on my best land ever since. There was no rest for me, no peace, no forgetfulness; turn where I would, there was his (Regent) cunning, grinning face at my elbow. It grew worse as Alice grew up, for he (Regent) soon saw I was more afraid of her (Alice) knowing my past than of the police. Whatever he (Regent) wanted he (Regent) must have, and whatever it was I gave him (Regent) without question, land, money, houses, until at last he (Regent) asked a thing which I could not give. He (Regent) asked for Alice.

``His (Regent) son, you see, had grown up, and so had my girl, and as I was known to be in weak health, it seemed a fine stroke to him (Regent) that his (Regent) lad should step into the whole property. But there I was firm. I would not have his (Regent) cursed stock mixed with mine; not that I had any dislike to the lad, but his (lad) blood was in him (lad), and that was enough. I stood firm. McCarthy threatened. I braved him (McCarthy) to do his (McCarthy) worst. We were to meet at the pool midway between our houses to talk it over.

``When I went down there I found him (McCarthy) talking with his (McCarthy) son, so I smoked a cigar and waited behind a tree until he (McCarthy) should be alone. But as I listened to his (McCarthy) talk all that was black and bitter in me seemed to come uppermost. He (McCarthy) was urging his (McCarthy) son to marry my daughter with as little regard for what she (daughter) might think as if she (daughter) were a slut from off the streets. It drove me mad to think that I and all that I held most dear should be in the power of such a man as this. Could I not snap the bond? I was already a dying and a desperate man. Though clear of mind and fairly strong of limb, I knew that my own fate was sealed. But my memory and my girl! Both could be saved if I could but silence that foul tongue. I did it, Mr. Holmes. I would do it again. Deeply as I have sinned, I have led a life of martyrdom to atone for it. But that my girl should be entangled in the same meshes which held me was more than I could suffer. I struck him (Holmes) down with no more compunction than if he (Holmes) had been some foul and venomous beast. His (Holmes) cry brought back his (Holmes) son; but I had gained the cover of the wood, though I was forced to go back to fetch the cloak which I had dropped in my flight. That is the true story, gentlemen, of all that occurred.'' (unknown)

``Well, it is not for me to judge you,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
said Holmes as the old man signed the statement which had been drawn out.

James McCarthy was acquitted at the Assizes on the strength of a number of objections which had been drawn out by Holmes and submitted to the defending counsel. Old Turner lived for seven months after our interview, but he (Turner) is now dead; and there is every prospect that the son and daughter may come to live happily together in ignorance of the black cloud which rests upon their past.

ADVENTURE V. THE FIVE ORANGE PIPS

When I glance over my notes and records of the Sherlock Holmes cases between the years '82 and '90, I am faced by so many which present strange and interesting features that it is no easy matter to know which to choose and which to leave. Some, however, have already gained publicity through the papers, and others have not offered a field for those peculiar qualities which my friend possessed in so high a degree, and which it is the object of these papers to illustrate. Some, too, have baffled his (friend) analytical skill, and would be, as narratives, beginnings without an ending, while others have been but partially cleared up, and have their explanations founded rather upon conjecture and surmise than on that absolute logical proof which was so dear to him (friend). There is, however, one of these last which was so remarkable in its details and so startling in its results that I am tempted to give some account of it in spite of the fact that there are points in connection with it which never have been, and probably never will be, entirely cleared up.

The year '87 furnished us with a long series of cases of greater or less interest, of which I retain the records. Among my headings under this one twelve months I find an account of the adventure of the Paradol Chamber, of the Amateur Mendicant Society, who held a luxurious club in the lower vault of a furniture warehouse, of the facts connected with the loss of the British barque

``Sophy Anderson'' (I)
, of the singular adventures of the Grice Patersons in the island of Uffa, and finally of the Camberwell poisoning case. In the latter, as may be remembered, Sherlock Holmes was able, by winding up the dead man's watch, to prove that it had been wound up two hours before, and that therefore the deceased had gone to bed within that time--a deduction which was of the greatest importance in clearing up the case. All these I may sketch out at some future date, but none of them present such singular features as the strange train of circumstances which I have now taken up my pen to describe.

It was in the latter days of September, and the equinoctial gales had set in with exceptional violence. All day the wind had screamed and the rain had beaten against the windows, so that even here in the heart of great, hand-made London we were forced to raise our minds for the instant from the routine of life and to recognise the presence of those great elemental forces which shriek at mankind through the bars of his (them) civilisation, like untamed beasts in a cage. As evening drew in, the storm grew higher and louder, and the wind cried and sobbed like a child in the chimney. Sherlock Holmes sat moodily at one side of the fireplace cross-indexing his (Holmes) records of crime, while I at the other was deep in one of Clark Russell's fine sea-stories until the howl of the gale from without seemed to blend with the text, and the splash of the rain to lengthen out into the long swash of the sea waves. My wife was on a visit to her (wife) mother's, and for a few days I was a dweller once more in my old quarters at Baker Street.

``Why,'' (I)
said I, glancing up at my companion,
``that was surely the bell. Who could come to-night? Some friend of yours, perhaps?'' (companion)

he (companion) answered.

Its power was used for political purposes, principally for the terrorising of the negro voters and the murdering and driving from the country of those who were opposed to its views. Its outrages were usually preceded by a warning sent to the marked man in some fantastic but generally recognised shape--a sprig of oak-leaves in some parts, melon seeds or orange pips in others. On receiving this the victim might either openly abjure his (victim) former ways, or might fly from the country. ADVENTURE VI. THE MAN WITH THE TWISTED LIP

Isa Whitney, brother of the late Elias Whitney, D.D., Principal of the Theological College of St. George's, was much addicted to opium. The habit grew upon him (George), as I understand, from some foolish freak when he (freak) was at college; for having read De Quincey's description of his (freak) dreams and sensations, he (freak) had drenched his (freak) tobacco with laudanum in an attempt to produce the same effects. He (freak) found, as so many more have done, that the practice is easier to attain than to get rid of, and for many years he (freak) continued to be a slave to the drug, an object of mingled horror and pity to his (freak) friends and relatives. I can see him (freak) now, with yellow, pasty face, drooping lids, and pin-point pupils, all huddled in a chair, the wreck and ruin of a noble man.

One night--it was in June, '89--there came a ring to my bell, about the hour when a man gives his (man) first yawn and glances at the clock. I sat up in my chair, and my wife laid her (wife) needle-work down in her (wife) lap and made a little face of disappointment.

``A patient!'' (she)
said she (wife).
``You'll have to go out.'' (I)

I groaned, for I was newly come back from a weary day.

We heard the door open, a few hurried words, and then quick steps upon the linoleum. Our own door flew open, and a lady, clad in some dark-coloured stuff, with a black veil, entered the room.

``You will excuse my calling so late,'' (she)
she (lady) began, and then, suddenly losing her (lady) self-control, she (lady) ran forward, threw her (lady) arms about my wife's neck, and sobbed upon her (lady) shoulder.
``Oh, I'm in such trouble!'' (she)
she (lady) cried;
``I do so want a little help.'' (she)

``Why,'' (wife Whitney)
said my wife, pulling up her (wife) veil,
``it is Kate Whitney. How you startled me, Kate! I had not an idea who you were when you came in.'' (wife Whitney)

``I didn't know what to do, so I came straight to you.'' (unknown)
That was always the way. Folk who were in grief came to my wife like birds to a light-house.

``It was very sweet of you to come. Now, you must have some wine and water, and sit here comfortably and tell us all about it. Or should you rather that I sent James off to bed?'' (unknown)

``Oh, no, no! I want the doctor's advice and help, too. It's about Isa. He (Isa) has not been home for two days. I am so frightened about him (Isa)!'' (she)

It was not the first time that she (wife) had spoken to us of her (wife) husband's trouble, to me as a doctor, to my wife as an old friend and school companion. We soothed and comforted her (wife) by such words as we could find. Did she (wife) know where her (wife) husband was? Was it possible that we could bring him (husband) back to her (wife)?

It seems that it was. She (wife) had the surest information that of late he (husband) had, when the fit was on him (husband), made use of an opium den in the farthest east of the City. Hitherto his (husband) orgies had always been confined to one day, and he (husband) had come back, twitching and shattered, in the evening. But now the spell had been upon him (husband) eight-and-forty hours, and he (husband) lay there, doubtless among the dregs of the docks, breathing in the poison or sleeping off the effects. There he (husband) was to be found, she (wife) was sure of it, at the Bar of Gold, in Upper Swandam Lane. But what was she (wife) to do? How could she (wife), a young and timid woman, make her (wife) way into such a place and pluck her (wife) husband out from among the ruffians who surrounded him (husband)?

There was the case, and of course there was but one way out of it. Might I not escort her (wife) to this place? And then, as a second thought, why should she (wife) come at all? I was Isa Whitney's medical adviser, and as such I had influence over him (Whitney). I could manage it better if I were alone. I promised her (wife) on my word that I would send him (Whitney) home in a cab within two hours if he (Whitney) were indeed at the address which she (wife) had given me. And so in ten minutes I had left my armchair and cheery sitting-room behind me, and was speeding eastward in a hansom on a strange errand, as it seemed to me at the time, though the future only could show how strange it was to be.

But there was no great difficulty in the first stage of my adventure. Upper Swandam Lane is a vile alley lurking behind the high wharves which line the north side of the river to the east of London Bridge. Between a slop-shop and a gin-shop, approached by a steep flight of steps leading down to a black gap like the mouth of a cave, I found the den of which I was in search. Ordering my cab to wait, I passed down the steps, worn hollow in the centre by the ceaseless tread of drunken feet; and by the light of a flickering oil-lamp above the door I found the latch and made my way into a long, low room, thick and heavy with the brown opium smoke, and terraced with wooden berths, like the forecastle of an emigrant ship.

Through the gloom one could dimly catch a glimpse of bodies lying in strange fantastic poses, bowed shoulders, bent knees, heads thrown back, and chins pointing upward, with here and there a dark, lack-lustre eye turned upon the newcomer. Out of the black shadows there glimmered little red circles of light, now bright, now faint, as the burning poison waxed or waned in the bowls of the metal pipes. The most lay silent, but some muttered to themselves, and others talked together in a strange, low, monotonous voice, their conversation coming in gushes, and then suddenly tailing off into silence, each mumbling out his (newcomer) own thoughts and paying little heed to the words of his (newcomer) neighbour. At the farther end was a small brazier of burning charcoal, beside which on a three-legged wooden stool there sat a tall, thin old man, with his (man) jaw resting upon his (man) two fists, and his (man) elbows upon his (man) knees, staring into the fire.

As I entered, a sallow Malay attendant had hurried up with a pipe for me and a supply of the drug, beckoning me to an empty berth.

``Thank you. I have not come to stay,'' (I.)
said I.
``There is a friend of mine here, Mr. Isa Whitney, and I wish to speak with him (Whitney).'' (I.)

There was a movement and an exclamation from my right, and peering through the gloom, I saw Whitney, pale, haggard, and unkempt, staring out at me.

``My God! It's Watson,'' (he)
said he (Whitney). He (Whitney) was in a pitiable state of reaction, with every nerve in a twitter.

``Nearly eleven.'' (unknown)

``Walk past me, and then look back at me.'' (I)

I whispered,
``what on earth are you doing in this den?'' (I)

``As low as you can,'' (he)
he (Holmes) answered;
``I have excellent ears. If you would have the great kindness to get rid of that sottish friend of yours I should be exceedingly glad to have a little talk with you.'' (he)

``Then pray send him (friend) home in it. You may safely trust him (friend), for he (friend) appears to be too limp to get into any mischief. I should recommend you also to send a note by the cabman to your wife to say that you have thrown in your lot with me. If you will wait outside, I shall be with you in five minutes.'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)

It was difficult to refuse any of Sherlock Holmes' requests, for they were always so exceedingly definite, and put forward with such a quiet air of mastery. I felt, however, that when Whitney was once confined in the cab my mission was practically accomplished; and for the rest, I could not wish anything better than to be associated with my friend in one of those singular adventures which were the normal condition of his (friend) existence. In a few minutes I had written my note, paid Whitney's bill, led him (Whitney) out to the cab, and seen him (Whitney) driven through the darkness. In a very short time a decrepit figure had emerged from the opium den, and I was walking down the street with Sherlock Holmes. For two streets he (Holmes) shuffled along with a bent back and an uncertain foot. Then, glancing quickly round, he (Holmes) straightened himself out and burst into a hearty fit of laughter.

``I suppose, Watson,'' (he)
said he (Holmes),
``that you imagine that I have added opium-smoking to cocaine injections, and all the other little weaknesses on which you have favoured me with your medical views.'' (he)

``I was certainly surprised to find you there.'' (unknown)

``But not more so than I to find you.'' (unknown)

``I came to find a friend.'' (unknown)

``And I to find an enemy.'' (unknown)

``An enemy?'' (unknown)

``Yes; one of my natural enemies, or, shall I say, my natural prey. Briefly, Watson, I am in the midst of a very remarkable inquiry, and I have hoped to find a clue in the incoherent ramblings of these sots, as I have done before now. Had I been recognised in that den my life would not have been worth an hour's purchase; for I have used it before now for my own purposes, and the rascally Lascar who runs it has sworn to have vengeance upon me. There is a trap-door at the back of that building, near the corner of Paul's Wharf, which could tell some strange tales of what has passed through it upon the moonless nights.'' (unknown)

A few country carts were stirring, bearing in vegetables to the metropolis, but the lines of villas on either side were as silent and lifeless as some city in a dream.

``It has been in some points a singular case,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
said Holmes, flicking the horse on into a gallop.
``I confess that I have been as blind as a mole, but it is better to learn wisdom late than never to learn it at all.'' (unknown)

In town the earliest risers were just beginning to look sleepily from their windows as we drove through the streets of the Surrey side. Passing down the Waterloo Bridge Road we crossed over the river, and dashing up Wellington Street wheeled sharply to the right and found ourselves in Bow Street. Sherlock Holmes was well known to the force, and the two constables at the door saluted him (Holmes). One of them held the horse's head while the other led us in.

``Who is on duty?'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
asked Holmes.

``Inspector Bradstreet, sir.'' (unknown)

``Ah, Bradstreet, how are you?'' (A official)
A tall, stout official had come down the stone-flagged passage, in a peaked cap and frogged jacket.

It was a small, office-like room, with a huge ledger upon the table, and a telephone projecting from the wall. The inspector sat down at his (inspector) desk.

``I called about that beggarman, Boone--the one who was charged with being concerned in the disappearance of Mr. Neville St. Clair, of Lee.'' (unknown)

He (inspector) led us down a passage, opened a barred door, passed down a winding stair, and brought us to a whitewashed corridor with a line of doors on each side.

``The third on the right is his (Lee),'' (the inspector)
said the inspector.
``Here it is!'' (He)
He (inspector) quietly shot back a panel in the upper part of the door and glanced through.

said he (inspector).
``You can see him (Lee) very well.'' (unknown)

We both put our eyes to the grating. The prisoner lay with his (prisoner) face towards us, in a very deep sleep, breathing slowly and heavily. He (prisoner) was a middle-sized man, coarsely clad as became his (prisoner) calling, with a coloured shirt protruding through the rent in his (prisoner) tattered coat. He (prisoner) was, as the inspector had said, extremely dirty, but the grime which covered his (inspector) face could not conceal its repulsive ugliness. A broad wheal from an old scar ran right across it from eye to chin, and by its contraction had turned up one side of the upper lip, so that three teeth were exposed in a perpetual snarl. A shock of very bright red hair grew low over his (lip) eyes and forehead.

``I had an idea that he (Lee) might, and I took the liberty of bringing the tools with me.'' (He)
He (Holmes) opened the Gladstone bag as he (Holmes) spoke, and took out, to my astonishment, a very large bath-sponge.

chuckled the inspector.

``Now, if you will have the great goodness to open that door very quietly, we will soon make him (Lee) cut a much more respectable figure.'' (unknown)

``Well, I don't know why not,'' (the inspector)

``He (Lee) doesn't look a credit to the Bow Street cells, does he (Lee)?'' (He)
He (inspector) slipped his (inspector) key into the lock, and we all very quietly entered the cell. The sleeper half turned, and then settled down once more into a deep slumber. Holmes stooped to the water-jug, moistened his (Holmes) sponge, and then rubbed it twice vigorously across and down the prisoner's face.

``Let me introduce you,'' (he)
he (Holmes) shouted,
``to Mr. Neville St. Clair, of Lee, in the county of Kent.'' (he)

Never in my life have I seen such a sight. The man's face peeled off under the sponge like the bark from a tree. Gone was the coarse brown tint! Gone, too, was the horrid scar which had seamed it across, and the twisted lip which had given the repulsive sneer to the face! A twitch brought away the tangled red hair, and there, sitting up in his (lip) bed, was a pale, sad-faced, refined-looking man, black-haired and smooth-skinned, rubbing his (lip) eyes and staring about him (lip) with sleepy bewilderment. ``Great heavens!'' (the inspector)
cried the inspector,
``it is, indeed, the missing man. I know him (man) from the photograph.'' (the inspector)

The prisoner turned with the reckless air of a man who abandons himself to his (man) destiny.

``Be it so,'' (he)
said he (man).
``And pray what am I charged with?'' (unknown)

``With making away with Mr. Neville St.-- Oh, come, you can't be charged with that unless they make a case of attempted suicide of it,'' (the inspector)
said the inspector with a grin.
``Well, I have been twenty-seven years in the force, but this really takes the cake.'' (unknown)

``If I am Mr. Neville St. Clair, then it is obvious that no crime has been committed, and that, therefore, I am illegally detained.'' (unknown)

Sherlock Holmes sat down beside him (Holmes) on the couch and patted him (Holmes) kindly on the shoulder.

said he (Holmes),
``of course you can hardly avoid publicity. On the other hand, if you convince the police authorities that there is no possible case against you, I do not know that there is any reason that the details should find their way into the papers. Inspector Bradstreet would, I am sure, make notes upon anything which you might tell us and submit it to the proper authorities. The case would then never go into court at all.'' (he)

``God bless you!'' (the prisoner)
cried the prisoner passionately. ``I would have endured imprisonment, ay, even execution, rather than have left my miserable secret as a family blot to my children.

``You are the first who have ever heard my story. My father was a schoolmaster in Chesterfield, where I received an excellent education. I travelled in my youth, took to the stage, and finally became a reporter on an evening paper in London. One day my editor wished to have a series of articles upon begging in the metropolis, and I volunteered to supply them. There was the point from which all my adventures started. It was only by trying begging as an amateur that I could get the facts upon which to base my articles. When an actor I had, of course, learned all the secrets of making up, and had been famous in the green-room for my skill. I took advantage now of my attainments. I painted my face, and to make myself as pitiable as possible I made a good scar and fixed one side of my lip in a twist by the aid of a small slip of flesh-coloured plaster. Then with a red head of hair, and an appropriate dress, I took my station in the business part of the city, ostensibly as a match-seller but really as a beggar. For seven hours I plied my trade, and when I returned home in the evening I found to my surprise that I had received no less than 26s. 4d.

``I wrote my articles and thought little more of the matter until, some time later, I backed a bill for a friend and had a writ served upon me for 25 pounds. I was at my wit's end where to get the money, but a sudden idea came to me. I begged a fortnight's grace from the creditor, asked for a holiday from my employers, and spent the time in begging in the City under my disguise. In ten days I had the money and had paid the debt.

``Well, you can imagine how hard it was to settle down to arduous work at 2 pounds a week when I knew that I could earn as much in a day by smearing my face with a little paint, laying my cap on the ground, and sitting still. It was a long fight between my pride and the money, but the dollars won at last, and I threw up reporting and sat day after day in the corner which I had first chosen, inspiring pity by my ghastly face and filling my pockets with coppers. Only one man knew my secret. He (man) was the keeper of a low den in which I used to lodge in Swandam Lane, where I could every morning emerge as a squalid beggar and in the evenings transform myself into a well-dressed man about town. This fellow, a Lascar, was well paid by me for his (fellow) rooms, so that I knew that my secret was safe in his (fellow) possession.

``Well, very soon I found that I was saving considerable sums of money. I do not mean that any beggar in the streets of London could earn 700 pounds a year--which is less than my average takings--but I had exceptional advantages in my power of making up, and also in a facility of repartee, which improved by practice and made me quite a recognised character in the City. All day a stream of pennies, varied by silver, poured in upon me, and it was a very bad day in which I failed to take 2 pounds.

``As I grew richer I grew more ambitious, took a house in the country, and eventually married, without anyone having a suspicion as to my real occupation. My dear wife knew that I had business in the City. She (wife) little knew what.

``Last Monday I had finished for the day and was dressing in my room above the opium den when I looked out of my window and saw, to my horror and astonishment, that my wife was standing in the street, with her (wife) eyes fixed full upon me. I gave a cry of surprise, threw up my arms to cover my face, and, rushing to my confidant, the Lascar, entreated him (confidant) to prevent anyone from coming up to me. I heard her (wife) voice downstairs, but I knew that she (wife) could not ascend. Swiftly I threw off my clothes, pulled on those of a beggar, and put on my pigments and wig. Even a wife's eyes could not pierce so complete a disguise. But then it occurred to me that there might be a search in the room, and that the clothes might betray me. I threw open the window, reopening by my violence a small cut which I had inflicted upon myself in the bedroom that morning. Then I seized my coat, which was weighted by the coppers which I had just transferred to it from the leather bag in which I carried my takings. I hurled it out of the window, and it disappeared into the Thames. The other clothes would have followed, but at that moment there was a rush of constables up the stair, and a few minutes after I found, rather, I confess, to my relief, that instead of being identified as Mr. Neville St. Clair, I was arrested as his (Clair) murderer.

``I do not know that there is anything else for me to explain. I was determined to preserve my disguise as long as possible, and hence my preference for a dirty face. Knowing that my wife would be terribly anxious, I slipped off my ring and confided it to the Lascar at a moment when no constable was watching me, together with a hurried scrawl, telling her (wife) that she (wife) had no cause to fear.'' (unknown)

``That note only reached her (wife) yesterday,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
said Holmes.

``Good God! What a week she (wife) must have spent!'' (unknown)

``The police have watched this Lascar,'' (Bradstreet/Inspector Bradstreet)
said Inspector Bradstreet,
``and I can quite understand that he (God) might find it difficult to post a letter unobserved. Probably he (God) handed it to some sailor customer of his (God), who forgot all about it for some days.'' (Bradstreet/Inspector Bradstreet)

``That was it,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
said Holmes, nodding approvingly;
``I have no doubt of it. But have you never been prosecuted for begging?'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)

``Many times; but what was a fine to me?'' (unknown)

``It must stop here, however,'' (Bradstreet/Inspector Bradstreet)
said Bradstreet.
``If the police are to hush this thing up, there must be no more of Hugh Boone.'' (unknown)

``I have sworn it by the most solemn oaths which a man can take.'' (unknown)

``In that case I think that it is probable that no further steps may be taken. But if you are found again, then all must come out. I am sure, Mr. Holmes, that we are very much indebted to you for having cleared the matter up. I wish I knew how you reach your results.'' (unknown)

``I reached this one,'' (friend)
said my friend,
``by sitting upon five pillows and consuming an ounce of shag. I think, Watson, that if we drive to Baker Street we shall just be in time for breakfast.'' (friend)

VII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE BLUE CARBUNCLE

I had called upon my friend Sherlock Holmes upon the second morning after Christmas, with the intention of wishing him (Holmes) the compliments of the season. He (Holmes) was lounging upon the sofa in a purple dressing-gown, a pipe-rack within his (Holmes) reach upon the right, and a pile of crumpled morning papers, evidently newly studied, near at hand. Beside the couch was a wooden chair, and on the angle of the back hung a very seedy and disreputable hard-felt hat, much the worse for wear, and cracked in several places. A lens and a forceps lying upon the seat of the chair suggested that the hat had been suspended in this manner for the purpose of examination.

``You are engaged,'' (I)
said I;
``perhaps I interrupt you.'' (I)

``Not at all. I am glad to have a friend with whom I can discuss my results. The matter is a perfectly trivial one'' (he)
--he (Holmes) jerked his (Holmes) thumb in the direction of the old hat--
``but there are points in connection with it which are not entirely devoid of interest and even of instruction.'' (he)

I seated myself in his (Holmes) armchair and warmed my hands before his (Holmes) crackling fire, for a sharp frost had set in, and the windows were thick with the ice crystals.

``I suppose,'' (I)
I remarked,
``that, homely as it looks, this thing has some deadly story linked on to it--that it is the clue which will guide you in the solution of some mystery and the punishment of some crime.'' (I)

``No, no. No crime,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
said Sherlock Holmes, laughing.
``Only one of those whimsical little incidents which will happen when you have four million human beings all jostling each other within the space of a few square miles. Amid the action and reaction of so dense a swarm of humanity, every possible combination of events may be expected to take place, and many a little problem will be presented which may be striking and bizarre without being criminal. We have already had experience of such.'' (unknown)

``So much so,'' (I)
I remarked,
``that of the last six cases which I have added to my notes, three have been entirely free of any legal crime.'' (I)

``Precisely. You allude to my attempt to recover the Irene Adler papers, to the singular case of Miss Mary Sutherland, and to the adventure of the man with the twisted lip. Well, I have no doubt that this small matter will fall into the same innocent category. You know Peterson, the commissionaire?'' (unknown)

``Yes.'' (unknown)

``It is to him (Peterson) that this trophy belongs.'' (unknown)

``It is his (Peterson) hat.'' (unknown)

``No, no, he (Peterson) found it. Its owner is unknown. I beg that you will look upon it not as a battered billycock but as an intellectual problem. And, first, as to how it came here. It arrived upon Christmas morning, in company with a good fat goose, which is, I have no doubt, roasting at this moment in front of Peterson's fire. The facts are these: about four o'clock on Christmas morning, Peterson, who, as you know, is a very honest fellow, was returning from some small jollification and was making his (fellow) way homeward down Tottenham Court Road. In front of him (fellow) he (fellow) saw, in the gaslight, a tallish man, walking with a slight stagger, and carrying a white goose slung over his (fellow) shoulder. As he (fellow) reached the corner of Goodge Street, a row broke out between this stranger and a little knot of roughs. One of the latter knocked off the man's hat, on which he (man) raised his (man) stick to defend himself and, swinging it over his (man) head, smashed the shop window behind him (man). Peterson had rushed forward to protect the stranger from his (Peterson) assailants; but the man, shocked at having broken the window, and seeing an official-looking person in uniform rushing towards him (man), dropped his (man) goose, took to his (man) heels, and vanished amid the labyrinth of small streets which lie at the back of Tottenham Court Road. The roughs had also fled at the appearance of Peterson, so that he (Peterson) was left in possession of the field of battle, and also of the spoils of victory in the shape of this battered hat and a most unimpeachable Christmas goose.'' (unknown)

``Which surely he (Peterson) restored to their owner?'' (unknown)

``My dear fellow, there lies the problem. It is true that ``For Mrs. Henry Baker' was printed upon a small card which was tied to the bird's left leg, and it is also true that the initials

``H. B.'' (Mrs. Henry Baker)
are legible upon the lining of this hat, but as there are some thousands of Bakers, and some hundreds of Henry Bakers in this city of ours, it is not easy to restore lost property to any one of them.''

``What, then, did Peterson do?'' (unknown)

``He (Peterson) brought round both hat and goose to me on Christmas morning, knowing that even the smallest problems are of interest to me. The goose we retained until this morning, when there were signs that, in spite of the slight frost, it would be well that it should be eaten without unnecessary delay. Its finder has carried it off, therefore, to fulfil the ultimate destiny of a goose, while I continue to retain the hat of the unknown gentleman who lost his (gentleman) Christmas dinner.'' (unknown)

``Did he (gentleman) not advertise?'' (unknown)

``No.'' (unknown)

``Then, what clue could you have as to his (gentleman) identity?'' (unknown)

``Only as much as we can deduce.'' (unknown)

``From his (gentleman) hat?'' (unknown)

``Precisely.'' (unknown)

``But you are joking. What can you gather from this old battered felt?'' (unknown)

``Here is my lens. You know my methods. What can you gather yourself as to the individuality of the man who has worn this article?'' (I)

I took the tattered object in my hands and turned it over rather ruefully. It was a very ordinary black hat of the usual round shape, hard and much the worse for wear. The lining had been of red silk, but was a good deal discoloured. There was no maker's name; but, as Holmes had remarked, the initials

``H. B.'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
were scrawled upon one side. It was pierced in the brim for a hat-securer, but the elastic was missing. For the rest, it was cracked, exceedingly dusty, and spotted in several places, although there seemed to have been some attempt to hide the discoloured patches by smearing them with ink.

``I can see nothing,'' (I)
said I, handing it back to my friend.

``On the contrary, Watson, you can see everything. You fail, however, to reason from what you see. You are too timid in drawing your inferences.'' (unknown)

``Then, pray tell me what it is that you can infer from this hat?'' (He)

He (friend) picked it up and gazed at it in the peculiar introspective fashion which was characteristic of him (friend).

``It is perhaps less suggestive than it might have been,'' (he)
he (friend) remarked,
``and yet there are a few inferences which are very distinct, and a few others which represent at least a strong balance of probability. That the man was highly intellectual is of course obvious upon the face of it, and also that he (man) was fairly well-to-do within the last three years, although he (man) has now fallen upon evil days. He (man) had foresight, but has less now than formerly, pointing to a moral retrogression, which, when taken with the decline of his (man) fortunes, seems to indicate some evil influence, probably drink, at work upon him (man). This may account also for the obvious fact that his (man) wife has ceased to love him (man).'' (he)

``My dear Holmes!'' (unknown)

``He (Holmes) has, however, retained some degree of self-respect,'' (he)
he (friend) continued, disregarding my remonstrance.
``He (Holmes) is a man who leads a sedentary life, goes out little, is out of training entirely, is middle-aged, has grizzled hair which he (Holmes) has had cut within the last few days, and which he (Holmes) anoints with lime-cream. These are the more patent facts which are to be deduced from his (Holmes) hat. Also, by the way, that it is extremely improbable that he (Holmes) has gas laid on in his (Holmes) house.'' (unknown)

``You are certainly joking, Holmes.'' (unknown)

``Not in the least. Is it possible that even now, when I give you these results, you are unable to see how they are attained?'' (unknown)

``I have no doubt that I am very stupid, but I must confess that I am unable to follow you. For example, how did you deduce that this man was intellectual?'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)

For answer Holmes clapped the hat upon his (Holmes) head. It came right over the forehead and settled upon the bridge of his (Holmes) nose.

``It is a question of cubic capacity,'' (he)
said he (Holmes);
``a man with so large a brain must have something in it.'' (he)

``The decline of his (man) fortunes, then?'' (unknown)

``This hat is three years old. These flat brims curled at the edge came in then. It is a hat of the very best quality. Look at the band of ribbed silk and the excellent lining. If this man could afford to buy so expensive a hat three years ago, and has had no hat since, then he (man) has assuredly gone down in the world.'' (unknown)

``Well, that is clear enough, certainly. But how about the foresight and the moral retrogression?'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)

Sherlock Holmes laughed.

``Here is the foresight,'' (he)
said he (Holmes) putting his (Holmes) finger upon the little disc and loop of the hat-securer.
``They are never sold upon hats. If this man ordered one, it is a sign of a certain amount of foresight, since he (man) went out of his (man) way to take this precaution against the wind. But since we see that he (man) has broken the elastic and has not troubled to replace it, it is obvious that he (man) has less foresight now than formerly, which is a distinct proof of a weakening nature. On the other hand, he (man) has endeavoured to conceal some of these stains upon the felt by daubing them with ink, which is a sign that he (man) has not entirely lost his (man) self-respect.'' (unknown)

``Your reasoning is certainly plausible.'' (unknown)

``The further points, that he (man) is middle-aged, that his (man) hair is grizzled, that it has been recently cut, and that he (man) uses lime-cream, are all to be gathered from a close examination of the lower part of the lining. The lens discloses a large number of hair-ends, clean cut by the scissors of the barber. They all appear to be adhesive, and there is a distinct odour of lime-cream. This dust, you will observe, is not the gritty, grey dust of the street but the fluffy brown dust of the house, showing that it has been hung up indoors most of the time, while the marks of moisture upon the inside are proof positive that the wearer perspired very freely, and could therefore, hardly be in the best of training.'' (unknown)

``But his (barber) wife--you said that she (wife) had ceased to love him (barber).'' (unknown)

``This hat has not been brushed for weeks. When I see you, my dear Watson, with a week's accumulation of dust upon your hat, and when your wife allows you to go out in such a state, I shall fear that you also have been unfortunate enough to lose your wife's affection.'' (unknown)

``But he (Watson) might be a bachelor.'' (unknown)

``Nay, he (Watson) was bringing home the goose as a peace-offering to his (Watson) wife. Remember the card upon the bird's leg.'' (unknown)

``You have an answer to everything. But how on earth do you deduce that the gas is not laid on in his (Watson) house?'' (unknown)

``One tallow stain, or even two, might come by chance; but when I see no less than five, I think that there can be little doubt that the individual must be brought into frequent contact with burning tallow--walks upstairs at night probably with his (Watson) hat in one hand and a guttering candle in the other. Anyhow, he (Watson) never got tallow-stains from a gas-jet. Are you satisfied?'' (unknown)

``Well, it is very ingenious,'' (I)
said I, laughing;
``but since, as you said just now, there has been no crime committed, and no harm done save the loss of a goose, all this seems to be rather a waste of energy.'' (I)

Sherlock Holmes had opened his (Holmes) mouth to reply, when the door flew open, and Peterson, the commissionaire, rushed into the apartment with flushed cheeks and the face of a man who is dazed with astonishment.

``The goose, Mr. Holmes! The goose, sir!'' (he)
he (man) gasped.

``Eh? What of it, then? Has it returned to life and flapped off through the kitchen window?'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)
Holmes twisted himself round upon the sofa to get a fairer view of the man's excited face.

``See here, sir! See what my wife found in its crop!'' (He)
He (Holmes) held out his (Holmes) hand and displayed upon the centre of the palm a brilliantly scintillating blue stone, rather smaller than a bean in size, but of such purity and radiance that it twinkled like an electric point in the dark hollow of his (Holmes) hand.

Sherlock Holmes sat up with a whistle.

``By Jove, Peterson!'' (he)
said he (Holmes),
``this is treasure trove indeed. I suppose you know what you have got?'' (he)

``A diamond, sir? A precious stone. It cuts into glass as though it were putty.'' (unknown)

``It's more than a precious stone. It is the precious stone.'' (unknown)

``Not the Countess of Morcar's blue carbuncle!'' (I)
I ejaculated.

``Precisely so. I ought to know its size and shape, seeing that I have read the advertisement about it in The Times every day lately. It is absolutely unique, and its value can only be conjectured, but the reward offered of 1000 pounds is certainly not within a twentieth part of the market price.'' (unknown)

``A thousand pounds! Great Lord of mercy!'' (unknown)
The commissionaire plumped down into a chair and stared from one to the other of us.

``That is the reward, and I have reason to know that there are sentimental considerations in the background which would induce the Countess to part with half her (Countess) fortune if she (Countess) could but recover the gem.'' (unknown)

``It was lost, if I remember aright, at the Hotel Cosmopolitan,'' (I)
I remarked.

``Precisely so, on December 22nd, just five days ago. John Horner, a plumber, was accused of having abstracted it from the lady's jewel-case. The evidence against him (Horner) was so strong that the case has been referred to the Assizes. I have some account of the matter here, I believe.'' (He)
He (Holmes) rummaged amid his (Holmes) newspapers, glancing over the dates, until at last he (Holmes) smoothed one out, doubled it over, and read the following paragraph:

``Hotel Cosmopolitan Jewel Robbery. John Horner, 26, plumber, was brought up upon the charge of having upon the 22nd inst., abstracted from the jewel-case of the Countess of Morcar the valuable gem known as the blue carbuncle. James Ryder, upper-attendant at the hotel, gave his (Ryder) evidence to the effect that he (Ryder) had shown Horner up to the dressing-room of the Countess of Morcar upon the day of the robbery in order that he (Ryder) might solder the second bar of the grate, which was loose. He (Ryder) had remained with Horner some little time, but had finally been called away. On returning, he (Horner) found that Horner had disappeared, that the bureau had been forced open, and that the small morocco casket in which, as it afterwards transpired, the Countess was accustomed to keep her (Countess) jewel, was lying empty upon the dressing-table. Ryder instantly gave the alarm, and Horner was arrested the same evening; but the stone could not be found either upon his (Horner) person or in his (Horner) rooms. Catherine Cusack, maid to the Countess, deposed to having heard Ryder's cry of dismay on discovering the robbery, and to having rushed into the room, where she (Countess) found matters as described by the last witness. Inspector Bradstreet, B division, gave evidence as to the arrest of Horner, who struggled frantically, and protested his (Horner) innocence in the strongest terms. Evidence of a previous conviction for robbery having been given against the prisoner, the magistrate refused to deal summarily with the offence, but referred it to the Assizes. Horner, who had shown signs of intense emotion during the proceedings, fainted away at the conclusion and was carried out of court.'' (he)

He (dealer) burst into convulsive sobbing, with his (dealer) face buried in his (dealer) hands.

``Get out!'' (he)
said he (friend).

``What, sir! Oh, Heaven bless you!'' (unknown)

``No more words. Get out!'' (unknown)

And no more words were needed. There was a rush, a clatter upon the stairs, the bang of a door, and the crisp rattle of running footfalls from the street.

``After all, Watson,'' (Holmes/Sherlock Holmes/Mr. Holmes)

VIII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE SPECKLED BAND

On glancing over my notes of the seventy odd cases in which I have during the last eight years studied the methods of my friend Sherlock Holmes, I find many tragic, some comic, a large number merely strange, but none commonplace; for, working as he (Holmes) did rather for the love of his (Holmes) art than for the acquirement of wealth, he (Holmes) refused to associate himself with any investigation which did not tend towards the unusual, and even the fantastic. Of all these varied cases, however, I cannot recall any which presented more singular features than that which was associated with the well-known Surrey family of the Roylotts of Stoke Moran. The events in question occurred in the early days of my association with Holmes, when we were sharing rooms as bachelors in Baker Street. It is possible that I might have placed them upon record before, but a promise of secrecy was made at the time, from which I have only been freed during the last month by the untimely death of the lady to whom the pledge was given. It is perhaps as well that the facts should now come to light, for I have reasons to know that there are widespread rumours as to the death of Dr. Grimesby Roylott which tend to make the matter even more terrible than the truth.

It was early in April in the year '83 that I woke one morning to find Sherlock Holmes standing, fully dressed, by the side of my bed. He (Holmes) was a late riser, as a rule, and as the clock on the mantelpiece showed me that it was only a quarter-past seven, I blinked up at him (Holmes) in some surprise, and perhaps just a little resentment, for I was myself regular in my habits.

``Very sorry to knock you up, Watson,'' (he)
said he (Holmes),
``but it's the common lot this morning. Mrs. Hudson has been knocked up, she (Hudson) retorted upon me, and I on you.'' (he)

``What is it, then--a fire?'' (unknown)

``No; a client. It seems that a young lady has arrived in a considerable state of excitement, who insists upon seeing me. She (lady) is waiting now in the sitting-room. Now, when young ladies wander about the metropolis at this hour of the morning, and knock sleepy people up out of their beds, I presume that it is something very pressing which they have to communicate. Should it prove to be an interesting case, you would, I am sure, wish to follow it from the outset. I thought, at any rate, that I should call you and give you the chance.'' (unknown)

``My dear fellow, I would not miss it for anything.'' (I)

I had no keener pleasure than in following Holmes in his (Holmes) professional investigations, and in admiring the rapid deductions, as swift as intuitions, and yet always founded on a logical basis with which he (Holmes) unravelled the problems which were submitted to him (Holmes). I rapidly threw on my clothes and was ready in a few minutes to accompany my friend down to the sitting-room. said the woman in a low voice, changing her (woman) seat as requested.

``What, then?'' (unknown)

Her (woman) features and figure were those of a woman of thirty, but her (woman) hair was shot with premature grey, and her (woman) expression was weary and haggard. Sherlock Holmes ran her (woman) over with one of his (Holmes) quick, all-comprehensive glances.

``You must not fear,'' (he)
said he (Holmes) soothingly, bending forward and patting her (woman) forearm.
``We shall soon set matters right, I have no doubt. You have come in by train this morning, I see.'' (unknown)

``No, but I observe the second half of a return ticket in the palm of your left glove. You must have started early, and yet you had a good drive in a dog-cart, along heavy roads, before you reached the station.'' (The lady)

The lady gave a violent start and stared in bewilderment at my companion.

said he (companion), smiling.

``But a terrible change came over our stepfather about this time. ``Last week he (stepfather) hurled the local blacksmith over a parapet into a stream, and it was only by paying over all the money which I could gather together that I was able to avert another public exposure. ``You can imagine from what I say that my poor sister Julia and I had no great pleasure in our lives. No servant would stay with us, and for a long time we did all the work of the house. ``Your sister is dead, then?'' (unknown)

``She (sister) died just two years ago, and it is of her (sister) death that I wish to speak to you. She (you) smiled back at me, closed my door, and a few moments later I heard her (you) key turn in the lock.''

``I could not sleep that night. A vague feeling of impending misfortune impressed me. '' There was something else which she (you) would fain have said, and she (you) stabbed with her (you) finger into the air in the direction of the doctor's room, but a fresh convulsion seized her (you) and choked her (you) words. The rapidity with which such a poison would take effect would also, from his (man) point of view, be an advantage. ``And also with the result of causing it to turn upon its master at the other side. Some of the blows of my cane came home and roused its snakish temper, so that it flew upon the first person it saw. In this way I am no doubt indirectly responsible for Dr. Grimesby Roylott's death, and I cannot say that it is likely to weigh very heavily upon my conscience.'' (unknown)

IX. THE ADVENTURE OF THE ENGINEER``S THUMB

Of all the problems which have been submitted to my friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, for solution during the years of our intimacy, there were only two which I was the means of introducing to his (Holmes) notice--that of Mr. Hatherley's thumb, and that of Colonel Warburton's madness. Of these the latter may have afforded a finer field for an acute and original observer, but the other was so strange in its inception and so dramatic in its details that it may be the more worthy of being placed upon record, even if it gave my friend fewer openings for those deductive methods of reasoning by which he (friend) achieved such remarkable results. The story has, I believe, been told more than once in the newspapers, but, like all such narratives, its effect is much less striking when set forth en bloc in a single half-column of print than when the facts slowly evolve before your own eyes, and the mystery clears gradually away as each new discovery furnishes a step which leads on to the complete truth. At the time the circumstances made a deep impression upon me, and the lapse of two years has hardly served to weaken the effect.

It was in the summer of '89, not long after my marriage, that the events occurred which I am now about to summarise. I had returned to civil practice and had finally abandoned Holmes in his (Holmes) Baker Street rooms, although I continually visited him (Holmes) and occasionally even persuaded him (Holmes) to forgo his (Holmes) Bohemian habits so far as to come and visit us. My practice had steadily increased, and as I happened to live at no very great distance from Paddington Station, I got a few patients from among the officials. One of these, whom I had cured of a painful and lingering disease, was never weary of advertising my virtues and of endeavouring to send me on every sufferer over whom he (sufferer) might have any influence.

One morning, at a little before seven o'clock, I was awakened by the maid tapping at the door to announce that two men had come from Paddington and were waiting in the consulting-room. I dressed hurriedly, for I knew by experience that railway cases were seldom trivial, and hastened downstairs. As I descended, my old ally, the guard, came out of the room and closed the door tightly behind him (guard).

``I've got him (guard) here,'' (he)
he (victim) whispered, jerking his (victim) thumb over his (victim) shoulder;
``he (guard)'s all right.'' (he)

``It's a new patient,'' (he)
he (victim) whispered.

``I took the lamp from him (colonel), and I examined the machine very thoroughly. It was indeed a gigantic one, and capable of exercising enormous pressure. When I passed outside, however, and pressed down the levers which controlled it, I knew at once by the whishing sound that there was a slight leakage, which allowed a regurgitation of water through one of the side cylinders. An examination showed that one of the india-rubber bands which was round the head of a driving-rod had shrunk so as not quite to fill the socket along which it worked. This was clearly the cause of the loss of power, and I pointed it out to my companions, who followed my remarks very carefully and asked several practical questions as to how they should proceed to set it right. When I had made it clear to them, I returned to the main chamber of the machine and took a good look at it to satisfy my own curiosity. It was obvious at a glance that the story of the fuller's-earth was the merest fabrication, for it would be absurd to suppose that so powerful an engine could be designed for so inadequate a purpose. The walls were of wood, but the floor consisted of a large iron trough, and when I came to examine it I could see a crust of metallic deposit all over it. I had stooped and was scraping at this to see exactly what it was when I heard a muttered exclamation in German and saw the cadaverous face of the colonel looking down at me.

''`What are you doing there?'' (he)
he (you) asked.

``I felt angry at having been tricked by so elaborate a story as that which he (colonel) had told me. ``I was admiring your fuller

``s-earth,'' (I)
said I;
``I think that I should be better able to advise you as to your machine if I knew what the exact purpose was for which it was used.'' (I)

``The instant that I uttered the words I regretted the rashness of my speech. His (colonel) face set hard, and a baleful light sprang up in his (colonel) grey eyes.

''`Very well,'' (he)

``Hullo! Colonel! Let me out!'' (unknown)

``And then suddenly in the silence I heard a sound which sent my heart into my mouth. It was the clank of the levers and the swish of the leaking cylinder. He (Colonel) had set the engine at work. The ceiling was only a foot or two above my head, and with my hand upraised I could feel its hard, rough surface. Then it flashed through my mind that the pain of my death would depend very much upon the position in which I met it. If I lay on my face the weight would come upon my spine, and I shuddered to think of that dreadful snap. Easier the other way, perhaps; and yet, had I the nerve to lie and look up at that deadly black shadow wavering down upon me? Already I was unable to stand erect, when my eye caught something which brought a gush of hope back to my heart.

``I have said that though the floor and ceiling were of iron, the walls were of wood. The panel had closed again behind me, but the crash of the lamp, and a few moments afterwards the clang of the two slabs of metal, told me how narrow had been my escape.

Were it not for the ugly wound upon my hand, all that had passed during those dreadful hours might have been an evil dream.

And Holmes' fears came to be realised, for from that day to this no word has ever been heard either of the beautiful woman, the sinister German, or the morose Englishman. Early that morning a peasant had met a cart containing several people and some very bulky boxes driving rapidly in the direction of Reading, but there all traces of the fugitives disappeared, and even Holmes' ingenuity failed ever to discover the least clue as to their whereabouts.

The firemen had been much perturbed at the strange arrangements which they had found within, and still more so by discovering a newly severed human thumb upon a window-sill of the second floor. About sunset, however, their efforts were at last successful, and they subdued the flames, but not before the roof had fallen in, and the whole place been reduced to such absolute ruin that, save some twisted cylinders and iron piping, not a trace remained of the machinery which had cost our unfortunate acquaintance so dearly. Large masses of nickel and of tin were discovered stored in an out-house, but no coins were to be found, which may have explained the presence of those bulky boxes which have been already referred to.

How our hydraulic engineer had been conveyed from the garden to the spot where he (engineer) recovered his (engineer) senses might have remained forever a mystery were it not for the soft mould, which told us a very plain tale. He (engineer) had evidently been carried down by two persons, one of whom had remarkably small feet and the other unusually large ones. On the whole, it was most probable that the silent Englishman, being less bold or less murderous than his (Englishman) companion, had assisted the woman to bear the unconscious man out of the way of danger.
